Refutation of the Mu'attila and Batiniyya through 'Huwa'
Explains the context and refutation of existence-deniers by 'Huwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'Huwa' in Surat al-Ikhlas serves as a refutation of the Mu'attila and Batiniyya sects by affirming Allah's existence.

Core Idea
'Huwa' is a polemical tool: it affirms Allah's existence against those who deny it.

Surat al-Ikhlas is not merely a declaration of tawhid; each of its verses is a direct refutation of specific heretical sects. This lesson focuses on the very first word, 'Huwa,' and how it counters the Mu'attila and Batiniyya who deny Allah's existence or true attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the source passage describing the revelation of the sura and the function of 'Huwa'.
Pay attention to the context: the sura was revealed without sensory perception.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Huwa'
'Huwa' is interpreted as an indication of the existent being (al-mawjud), affirming that Allah truly exists.
This refutes those who deny existence or attributes.
3
Identify the refuted sects
The passage explicitly says that 'Huwa' refutes the Mu'attila and Batiniyya. Learn who they are.
Mu'attila deny Allah's attributes; Batiniyya deny apparent meanings and sometimes existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من غير رؤية وإحاطة(١)، فقال جل جلاله لرسوله : قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدٌ ﴾ [الإخلاص: ١] إلي آخر السورة (٢). و هُوَ ) إشارة إلى الموجود، ونقضّ على المعطلة والباطنية (۳)، (أحدٌ ) إثبات (٤) وحدته(٥)، ونقضّ على المشركين والثنوية، اللهُ الصَّمَدُ ) [الاخلاص : ٢] نقض على المشبهة لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدٌ ﴾ [الإخلاص ۳] نقض علي اليهود والنصارى وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ : كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ ﴾ نقضّ على المجوس بقولهم : يَزْدَان) وأَهْرَمَن» (٦) كما قال الله تعالي: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١].
Without seeing and encompassing (1), He, exalted be His majesty, said to His Messenger: Say, 'He is Allah, the One' (Surah al-Ikhlas: 1) to the end of the sura (2). And 'Huwa' is an indication to the Existent, and a refutation against the Mu'atțila and the Bāṭiniyya (3). 'Aḥad' is an affirmation of His oneness (4,5), and a refutation against the polytheists and the Dualists. 'Allāhuṣ Ṣamad' (al-Ikhlas: 2) is a refutation against the Mushabbiha. 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' (al-Ikhlas: 3) is a refutation against the Jews and Christians. 'Wa lam yakun lahū kufuwan aḥad' (al-Ikhlas: 4) is a refutation against the Magians by their saying 'Yazdān' and 'Ahriman' (6), as Allah the Exalted said: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing' (Surah ash-Shura: 11).
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how Surat al-Ikhlas refutes various deviant sects. The author begins by stating that without seeing and encompassing (a reference to the impossibility of perceiving Allah with the senses or comprehending Him fully), Allah revealed this sura to His Messenger. Each phrase of the sura targets a specific false belief: 'Huwa' indicates the existence of Allah, refuting those who deny His existence (Mu'atțila and Bāṭiniyya). 'Aḥad' affirms His oneness, refuting polytheists and dualists. 'Aṣ-Ṣamad' refutes those who anthropomorphize (Mushabbiha). 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refutes those who attribute offspring to Allah (Jews and Christians). 'Wa lam yakun lahū kufuwan aḥad' refutes the dualistic Magians who believe in two principles. The author concludes by citing the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' which is the general principle of transcendence.

Key Terms
المعطلة
الباطنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the sura's verses are merely declarative statements without polemical intent.
Correction: Each phrase, including 'Huwa', is a direct refutation of specific sects, as shown in the passage.

Checkpoint
Which sects are refuted by the word 'Huwa' in Surat al-Ikhlas?
A) Mu'attila and Batiniyya
B) Mushabbiha
C) Jews and Christians
D) Magians

Key Takeaway
The word 'Huwa' in Surat al-Ikhlas affirms Allah's existence and directly refutes the Mu'attila and Batiniyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself living as if Allah's existence is not the most certain reality in your life? When has that happened, and what would it mean for you to truly internalize that 'Huwa' indicates the Existent?
?
The word 'Huwa' in Surah al-Ikhlas is a direct refutation of those who deny Allah's existence. Examine your own heart: are there any subtle forms of denial or neglect of Allah's being in your daily actions? How can you use this verse to reaffirm His existence starting today?
?
If someone asked you why you are certain that Allah exists, how would your answer be different after learning that 'Huwa' itself is a polemical proof? How can you make that certainty more alive in your worship right now?

Affirmation of Oneness: Refuting Polytheists and Dualists
Explains how 'Ahad' refutes polytheists and dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'Ahad' in Surat al-Ikhlas affirms Allah's oneness and refutes polytheists and dualists.

Core Idea
'Ahad' is a declaration of exclusive oneness against all forms of dualism and polytheism.

The second key word in Surat al-Ikhlas is 'Ahad', which explicitly states that Allah is One. This lesson explains how this simple word refutes the polytheists (mushrikun) who associate partners with Allah, and the dualists (thanawiyya) who believe in two divine principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the phrase from the source that discusses the word 'Ahad'.
Notice that the passage says 'Ahad' affirms His oneness and refutes specific groups.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Ahad'
'Ahad' means 'One' in the sense of absolute uniqueness, with no partners or equals.
This oneness is both in essence and attributes.
3
Identify the refuted sects
The passage explicitly mentions polytheists (mushrikun) and dualists (thanawiyya).
Dualists include Zoroastrians and others who believe in two eternal principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that 'Ahad' is not a passive description but an active rejection of every partner—whether in worship, essence, or power. Look honestly at your life: what 'hidden partners' have you allowed to compete with Allah for your ultimate trust or loyalty? Name them, and commit to uprooting one today.
?
The lesson says that 'Ahad' refutes both polytheists and dualists—both those who worship many gods and those who divide ultimate reality into two. In your own heart, do you sometimes act as if there are two powers at work in your life: Allah and something else (fate, people, your own strength)? How does the oneness of Allah demand that you surrender that second source of control?
?
You have seen that the word 'Ahad' leaves no room for any partner—not even a subtle one like the belief that good and evil come from separate sources. Is there an area of your life where you silently blame a 'second force'—a person, circumstance, or even shaytan—for your failures, instead of turning wholly to Allah? What would it look like to repent of that dualism and submit entirely to His absolute oneness?

The Self-Sufficient: Refuting the Anthropomorphists
Explains how 'Samad' refutes the Mushabbiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'Samad' in Surat al-Ikhlas refutes the Mushabbiha who liken Allah to creation.

Core Idea
'Samad' describes Allah as self-sufficient and independent, refuting any likeness to creation.

The third verse of Surat al-Ikhlas describes Allah as 'Samad' (the Self-Sufficient, the Eternal Refuge). This lesson explains how this attribute refutes the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) who liken Allah to created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the phrase 'Allahu Samad' and its explanation from the passage.
Note that it is a refutation of the Mushabbiha.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Samad'
'Samad' means that Allah is self-sufficient, not needing anything, and all creation depends on Him.
This implies no similarity to creation.
3
Identify the refuted sect
The passage says 'Samad' refutes the Mushabbiha, who anthropomorphize Allah.
Mushabbiha attribute human-like qualities to Allah.

?

Transcendence of Birth: Refuting Jews and Christians
Explains how 'lam yalid wa lam yulad' refutes Jews and Christians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Lam yalid wa lam yulad' in Surat al-Ikhlas refutes Jewish and Christian claims about Allah having offspring.

Core Idea
'Lam yalid wa lam yulad' denies any genealogy for Allah, refuting those who claim He has a son.

The third verse of Surat al-Ikhlas emphatically states that Allah neither begets nor is begotten. This lesson explains how this verse refutes the Jews and Christians who attribute offspring to Allah (e.g., claiming Ezra is the son of God or Jesus is the son of God).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the verse and its explanation from the passage.
Note that it refutes Jews and Christians.
2
Understand the meaning of 'lam yalid wa lam yulad'
This negates any birth or being born, emphasizing that Allah is ungenerated and does not generate.
This refutes any claim of divine sonship.
3
Identify the refuted groups
The passage explicitly says this refutes the Jews and Christians.
Jews claimed Ezra is the son of God; Christians claimed Jesus is the son of God.

Source Text

The Incomparable: Refuting the Magians
Explains how the final verse refutes Magian dualism, supported by Quran 42:11.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final verse of Surat al-Ikhlas refutes the Magians' dualistic belief in two principles.

Core Idea
'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' denies any equal to Allah, refuting dualism.

The fourth and final verse of Surat al-Ikhlas declares that there is none comparable to Allah. This lesson explains how this verse refutes the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two opposing deities, Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the final verse and the concluding citation from Surah ash-Shura.
Notice the specific mention of the Magians and their dualistic terms.
2
Understand the meaning of the verse
The verse denies that anyone is equal or comparable to Allah.
This includes denying any partner or rival.
3
Learn about the Magian belief
The Magians believed in two eternal principles: Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil).
The verse refutes this by affirming Allah's absolute uniqueness.
4
Connect with the general principle
The passage concludes with Quran 42:11, 'There is nothing like unto Him', as a comprehensive refutation.
This reinforces the transcendence of Allah.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Magians were polytheists worshipping multiple gods, but they were dualists with two principles.
Correction: The Magians believed in two co-eternal principles, Yazdan and Ahriman, not multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
The final verse of Surat al-Ikhlas refutes dualism by affirming that nothing is comparable to Allah, directly countering the Magian belief in two principles.

Why Affirming a Body for Allah Entails Modality and Is Impossible
Nasafi presents a conditional argument against attributing a body to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a body (jism) to Allah necessarily entails modality (kayfiyyah), and why that is impossible for the Creator.

Core Idea
To say Allah has a body is to say He has a mode — and that is impossible.

Nasafi presents a concise but powerful argument against those who claim that Allah has a body. Such an affirmation is not just a matter of imagery; it logically entails modality, which contradicts divine transcendence. In this lesson, you will parse the argument and see why it is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: if A then B, and B is impossible.
2
Understand the definition of body
The passage refers to 'the definition of body' mentioned earlier. In kalam, body (jism) is defined as something composed of atoms and accidents, which requires modality.
If you are not familiar, the definition implies having dimensions and qualities.
3
See the conclusion
Because modality is impossible for Allah, affirming body is also impossible. The final phrase is a prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: إذا قلتم بالجسم فقد قلتم بالكيفية لما ذكرنا من حد الجسم، ولا يمكن إثباته في ذات الباري جَلّ جلاله، والله الهادي إلى سبيل الرشاد.
We say: If you affirm the body (al-jism), then you have affirmed modality (al-kayfiyyah) according to what we have mentioned of the definition of body, and it is impossible to establish it in the essence of the Creator, exalted is His majesty. And Allah is the Guide to the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an argument against those who attribute a body (jism) to Allah. Nasafi begins by stating a conditional: if you affirm that Allah has a body, then you necessarily affirm modality (kayfiyyah), because the definition of body includes having a certain quality or mode. This is based on the previously mentioned definition of body. He then concludes that it is impossible to establish a body in the essence of Allah, because modality implies createdness and limitation. The passage ends with a pious phrase.

Key Terms

الكيفية (al-kayfiyyah)
حد الجسم (definition of body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Nasafi shows that the very definition of body entails modality, which is inherently incompatible with Allah's transcendence, regardless of mental reservations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Affirming a body for Allah entails modality, which contradicts divine transcendence, so body cannot be attributed to Him.

Negating Likeness Does Not Negate Corporeality
Nasafi refutes those who affirm hand and foot for Allah by claiming it's tanzih, clarifying the distinction between likeness and corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming hand and foot for Allah while denying likeness is insufficient for tanzih, and why tanzih requires negating corporeality itself.

Core Idea
True tanzih requires negating both likeness and corporeality.

This passage corrects a common error: some think that ascribing literal hand and foot to Allah is acceptable as long as they deny similarity to human organs. Imam Nasafi shows the flaw in this reasoning, explaining the critical distinction between negating likeness (mithliyyah) and negating corporeality (jismiyyah).

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Understand the assertion that Allah has a hand and foot 'in reality' but not like human organs, and that this is claimed to be tanzih.
The opponent thinks negating likeness is enough for transcendence.
2
Identify Nasafi's critique
Nasafi points out the logical flaw: the opponent only negated mithliyyah (likeness), not jismiyyah (corporeality). Both must be negated for true tanzih.
Jismiyyah means being a body or having physical parts.
3
Note the explanation for created beings
Nasafi explains that such anthropomorphic descriptions (amthal) only apply to created beings that are composed of parts and subject to change.
This reinforces that Allah is free from all such limitations.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proof
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' negates any comparison, supporting the complete transcendence of Allah.
This is a decisive proof against any form of anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى يدًا حقيقة ورجلاً حقيقة ولكن ليست كأيدينا، زاعما أن هذا تنزيه الله تعالى، وهو في الحقيقة ليس كذلك، لأن هذا نفي للمثلية وليس نفيًا للجسمية، والله تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والمثلية، وهذه الأمثال قد تصح في حق المخلوقات المحدثة التي تتكون من أجزاء وأبعاض، وتطرأ عليها الأعراض والحوادث، تعالى الله عما يصفون لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ) [الشورى: ١١].
He [the opponent] says: He [Allah] is exalted, a hand in reality and a foot in reality, but not like our hands, claiming that this is tanzih (transcendence) of Allah, but in reality it is not so, because this is a negation of likeness (al-mithliyyah) and not a negation of corporeality (al-jismiyyah). And Allah is transcendent above corporeality and likeness. And these similes may be valid for created originated beings that consist of parts and members, and upon which accidents and events occur. Exalted is Allah above what they describe. 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (Quran 42:11).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage addresses a false claim that affirming literal hand and foot for Allah while negating likeness to human organs is a form of tanzih (declaring transcendence). Nasafi corrects this by distinguishing two concepts: mithliyyah (likeness) and jismiyyah (corporeality). The opponent's statement only negates mithliyyah, but does not negate jismiyyah. However, Allah is exalted above both jismiyyah and mithliyyah. Nasafi then explains that such anthropomorphic descriptions (amthal) might be valid for created beings that are composed of parts and subject to accidents, but not for Allah. He ends with the Quranic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' (Nothing is like Him), which negates any comparison.

Key Terms
المثلية (al-mithliyyah)
الجسمية (al-jismiyyah)
التنزيه (al-tanzih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming hand and foot for Allah while denying likeness is a valid form of tanzih.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this only negates likeness, not corporeality, and Allah is transcendent above both.

Checkpoint
What is the distinction Nasafi makes between mithliyyah and jismiyyah?
A) Mithliyyah is for created beings, jismiyyah is for Allah.
B) Mithliyyah refers to likeness, jismiyyah refers to being a body.
C) There is no distinction.
D) Jismiyyah is a type of mithliyyah.

Key Takeaway
True tanzih requires negating both corporeality and likeness from Allah, as no comparison can be made to His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself, in your heart, imagining Allah with human-like qualities even while your tongue denies similarity? What steps can you take today to purge those mental images?
?
The opponent thought his compromise was sufficient—where in your own understanding of Allah do you settle for half-truths or incomplete tanzih? Will you actively seek the full negation commanded by 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'?
?
Reflect on the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'. Does your conception of Allah truly align with this absolute negation, or do you unknowingly harbor subtle comparisons? Commit to one concrete change in how you think about Allah from now on.

Is Allah Called Light? Refuting Anthropomorphism
Nasafi refutes anthropomorphism regarding Allah being called light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah forbid calling Allah 'light' literally and how Quranic verses are interpreted to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Calling Allah 'light' literally implies anthropomorphism; He is the Creator and Illuminator of light.

This passage addresses a theological dispute: can Allah be called light? The Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) claim yes, citing the Quranic verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth.' However, Ahl al-Sunnah reject this literal interpretation, insisting that light is a created attribute. The text presents their reasoning and the proper understanding of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the contrasting positions of the two groups.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Find the claim of the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) and the counter-position of Ahl al-Sunnah. What is each group's stance?
3
Analyze the Quranic proof texts
Each side uses a Quranic verse. Identify the verse cited by the Mushabbiha and the verse cited by Ahl al-Sunnah. How do they support their arguments?
4
Understand the interpretive responses
The passage provides two interpretations of the key verse from Ibn Abbas and others. Note how these interpretations avoid literal attribution of light to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان هل يقال إن الله تعالى نور؟ قالت المشبهة لعنهم الله : يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى نور يتلألأ. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة رضي الله عنهم : لا يجوز ، بل هو خالق النور ومنوّر النور، لأن النور له لون، فلو قلنا بأنه نور يلزمنا التشبيه، والله تعالى منزّه عن التشبيه. قال تعالى: (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ) [الشورى: ١١] وهم احتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥] سمى نفسه نورا. والجواب عنه أن نقول : قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: يعني منوّر السموات والأرض، وقال بعضهم: يعني هادي أهلِ السَّموات والأرض.
Chapter on explaining whether it is said that Allah Most High is light. The Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists), may Allah curse them, said: It is permissible to say that Allah Most High is a shining light. The Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, may Allah be pleased with them, said: It is not permissible; rather, He is the Creator of light and the Illuminator of light, because light has a color. So if we say He is light, it necessitates anthropomorphism (tashbih), and Allah Most High is transcendent above anthropomorphism. He said: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (al-Shura 42:11). They argued with His saying: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' (al-Nur 24:35), He called Himself light. The answer to that is that we say: Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: He means the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth. And some said: He means the Guide of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation. It begins with the claim of the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) that Allah can be called a shining light. The author juxtaposes this with the position of Ahl al-Sunnah, who forbid this denomination because light is a created attribute with color, and applying it to Allah would imply similarity between the Creator and creation. The proof text is the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him.' The Mushabbiha in turn cite the verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' to support their claim. The author provides two answers from early authorities: Ibn Abbas interprets the verse as meaning Allah is the Illuminator, not light itself; another interpretation says He is the Guide. Both interpretations avoid the literal attribution of light to Allah, preserving divine transcendence.

مشبهة
أهل السنة والجماعة
منوّر
هادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' means Allah is literally light.
Correction: This passage shows that the verse must be interpreted metaphorically as 'Illuminator' or 'Guide' to avoid anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint

A) Because light is a created thing with color, leading to anthropomorphism.
B) Because the Quran does not use that term for Allah.
C) Because light is only used for prophets.
D) Because light implies darkness.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah maintain that Allah is not literal light but the Creator and Illuminator of light, interpreting the Quranic description metaphorically to preserve His transcendence.

Calling Allah 'Hand': Linguistic Permissibility
Nasafi gives a ruling on calling Allah 'hand' in Arabic vs. Persian.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is permissible to use the word 'hand' for Allah in Arabic but not in Persian, based on linguistic connotation.

Core Idea
Language matters: a safe term in Arabic can be problematic in another language due to different meanings.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling about divine names. The author, Imam Nasafi, explains that while calling Allah 'hand' (yad) is allowed in Arabic, the same is not true in Persian. This distinction highlights the importance of linguistic context in preserving divine transcendence. Let's examine the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise ruling.
The passage is very short; pay attention to the two clauses.
2
Understand the reasoning
Consider why Arabic permits it but Persian does not. In Arabic, 'yad' has figurative meanings (power, favor) and occurs in the Quran. In Persian, 'dast' is understood literally as a limb, leading to anthropomorphism.
This is not stated explicitly but is the underlying logic.
3
Reflect on the principle
Reflect on the broader methodological point: theological language must be sensitive to linguistic usage to avoid distorting Allah's transcendence.
This principle applies to other divine attributes as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان هل يقال الله يدًا؟ ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى يداً بالعربية، ولا يجوز بالفارسية.
Chapter on explaining whether Allah is called 'hand'? And it is permissible to say that Allah Most High is 'hand' in Arabic, but not permissible in Persian.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling on a divine name. The author states that calling Allah 'hand' (yad) is permissible in Arabic, but not in Persian. This distinction is based on the fact that in Arabic, the word 'yad' has a range of meanings including power, authority, and favor, and is used in the Quran (e.g., 'Yad Allahi fawqa aydihim' – Allah's hand is above their hands). However, in Persian, the word 'dast' (hand) is primarily understood as a physical limb, which would lead to anthropomorphism. Therefore, it is forbidden to use the Persian equivalent. The ruling underscores the importance of linguistic context in theology: a term that is safe in one language may be problematic in another due to different connotations.

Key Terms
يد
العربية
الفارسية
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If 'yad' is permissible in Arabic, it implies Allah has a literal hand.
Correction: The permissibility is linguistic; the meaning is metaphorical (power, favor), not literal.

Checkpoint

A) Because Arabic is the language of the Quran.
B) Because the Persian word 'dast' always means a physical hand, leading to anthropomorphism.
C) Because Arabic has no word for hand.
D) Because Persian speakers cannot understand the metaphor.

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah 'hand' is permissible in Arabic due to its figurative range, but forbidden in Persian to avoid anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Why 'Not Like Ours' Is Not Enough
The author argues that saying 'not like ours' does not negate corporeality and opens door to Satanic whisperings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why saying 'Allah has a hand or a leg, but not like ours' does not truly negate corporeality and can lead to anthropomorphic thinking.

Core Idea
Negating similarity does not negate corporeality—only negating modality (kayf) does.

This lesson examines a common but flawed approach to divine attributes: affirming a hand or leg while adding 'not like ours.' The author shows that this leaves the door open for imagining a body and for Satan to ask 'how?', leading to anthropomorphism. We'll see why the correct response is to stop at the texts and not engage in speculative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the author presents the flawed statement and then critiques it.
The passage includes a hadith analogy—pay attention to the comparison.
2
Identify the central claim
The author argues that negating similarity (tamāthul) does not necessarily negate corporeality (tajassum). Understand this logical point: something can be a body even if it is unlike any other body.
This is the key insight of the lesson.
3
Understand the danger of leaving modality open
The flawed statement only denies similarity of modalities (kayfiyyāt), not modality itself. Satan then exploits this by asking 'How is it?'—leading to false mental conceptions.
The author links this to the famous hadith about Satan's whispering.
4
Learn the proper response
The correct approach is to desist from such speculations and adhere to the revealed texts without delving into statements that lead to corporealism and anthropomorphism.
This reflects the Sunni principle of tafwīḍ (consigning the modality to Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك قول أحدهم إن الله يدا أو رجلاً ليست كأيدينا، معتقدا أنه بذلك قد نزّه المولى جل في علاه عن التجسم فهذا أيضًا غير صحيح، لأن نفي التماثل لا يعني بالضرورة نفي الجسمية، وهو بذلك لم يغلق باب التصورات العقلية الوهمية لذات الله تعالى، بل فتح بابًا لتسلّط الشيطان على عقله وقلبه، فقد نفى تماثل الكيفيات ولم ينف الكيفية أصلاً، والله تعالى منزّه عن الكيف، فقد يأتيه الشيطان ليقول له: إن لم تكن يد الله ليست كيدك، فكيف هي إذن؟ كما في الحديث المتفق عليه من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يأتي الشيطانُ أحدكم فيقول : من خلق كذا؟ من خلق كذا ؟ حتى يقول : من خلق الله ؟ فإذا بلغه فليقل: آمنت بالله ورسله فمثل هذه الأقوال الملبسة لا تغلق هذا الباب، بل تفتح باب التصورات العقلية الباطلة لذات الله تعالى، والواجب الانتهاء عن ذلك والوقوف مع النصوص التي وردت عن الله تعالى وعن رسوله، دون تورط في أقوال تفضي إلى التجسيم والتشبيه، والله أعلى وأعلم.
Similarly, the statement of someone that 'Allah has a hand or a leg not like our hands'—thinking that by doing so he has exonerated the Lord, Glorified in His Highness, from corporeality—this too is incorrect. Because the negation of similarity does not necessarily entail the negation of corporeality. By that he did not close the door of imaginary mental conceptions of Allah Most High, but rather opened a door for Satan to dominate his mind and heart. For he negated the similarity of modalities but did not negate modality itself at all, and Allah Most High is transcendent above modality. Satan may come to him and say: 'If the hand of Allah is not like your hand, then how is it?' As in the agreed-upon hadith from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Satan comes to one of you and says: Who created such-and-such? Who created such-and-such? Until he says: Who created Allah? When he reaches that, let him say: I believe in Allah and His Messengers.' So such confusing statements do not close this door, rather they open the door to false mental conceptions of Allah Most High. And it is obligatory to desist from that and to adhere to the texts that have come from Allah Most High and from His Messenger, without getting involved in statements that lead to corporealism and anthropomorphism. And Allah is Highest and Most Knowing.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage refutes an inadequate approach to divine attributes: saying 'Allah has a hand or a leg, but not like ours.' The author argues that this statement is insufficient because negating similarity (تماثل) does not necessarily negate corporeality (تجسم). One can still imagine a body, even if dissimilar. The key is that Allah is transcendent above modality (كيف) altogether. Such a statement leaves the door open for Satan to whisper: 'If His hand is not like yours, then how is it?' This is analogous to the hadith where Satan progressively leads a person to ask 'Who created Allah?' The proper response is to stop and affirm faith, not to engage in speculative reasoning. The author concludes that one must adhere to the revealed texts without delving into statements that inevitably lead to corporealism (تجسيم) and anthropomorphism (تشبيه).

Key Terms
تجسم (corporeality)
تماثل (similarity)
كيف (modality)
تسلط الشيطان (Satanic domination)
تجسيم (corporealism)
تشبيه (anthropomorphism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that saying 'Allah's hand is not like ours' sufficiently negates anthropomorphism and protects against corporeal conceptions.
Correction: As the passage explains, negating similarity does not negate corporeality. One may still imagine a body, only unlike ours. The correct approach is to negate modality (kayf) altogether and stop at the texts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with saying 'Allah's hand is not like ours'?
A) It is a correct statement but incomplete
B) It does not prevent imagining a body
C) It contradicts the Qur'an
D) It is a Mu'tazilite view

Key Takeaway
Saying 'Allah's hand is not like ours' is insufficient because it only denies similarity, not corporeality or modality; the correct stance is to affirm the texts without speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about Allah's hand or throne, do you ever catch yourself trying to imagine them, even if you quickly tell yourself they are 'not like anything'? What does this lesson reveal about the subtle danger of that habit? Will you now consciously stop and say 'I believe in Allah and His Messengers' whenever such thoughts arise?
?
Satan's whisper about 'how' Allah's attributes are is a direct consequence of leaving the door of speculation open. Have you fallen into the trap of thinking that your intellectual reasoning can protect you from false conceptions? What concrete step will you take today to shut that door and adhere strictly to the texts without 'how'?
?
The Prophet's cure for waswasa is to stop questioning and affirm faith. Is there a doubt about Allah's attributes that you have been entertaining in your heart? Will you now surrender it and say 'آمنت بالله ورسله' (I believe in Allah and His Messengers) as a final answer?

Refutation of the Mushabbiha on God's Hand
Nasafi presents and refutes the anthropomorphist claim that God has a physical hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anthropomorphist (Mushabbiha) claim that God has a physical hand and fingers, and how Nasafi exposes the flaw in their proof from the Quran.

Core Idea
God's hand is not a limb; the Quran refutes the Mushabbiha's literalism.

The Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) insisted that God has a physical form with hands and even fingers, citing a Quranic verse about the heavens being folded in His right hand. In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents their claim and their proof, setting the stage for a refutation grounded in divine transcendence (tanzih). Let's see the exact wording and the verse they misuse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the Mushabbiha's assertions.
Pay attention to the exaggerated details they attribute to Allah.
2
Identify the claims
Note that they attribute to God a form, two hands, both right, and even a shin and fingers.
These are all physical attributes that imply a body.
3
Examine their proof
They cite the verse from Az-Zumar: 'The earth all together is His handful... and the heavens are folded in His right hand.'
The verse appears to support their view literally.
4
Recognize the refutation
The verse itself ends with 'Glory be to Him above what they associate' (not in passage but context). Understand that the passage presents the claim to show its absurdity.
Nasafi implies that the verse's conclusion negates their interpretation.
5
Reflect on tanzih
Why is it necessary to deny physical attributes to God? This lesson emphasizes divine transcendence.
Tanzih is a core principle of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the anthropomorphist (Mushabbiha) claim that Allah has a physical form, two hands, and that both are right hands (since left is deficient), and that He has a shin and fingers. They support their position by citing the verse from Surah Az-Zumar: 'And the earth all together is His handful on the Day of Resurrection, and the heavens are folded in His right hand.' Nasafi's refutation is not explicitly stated in this excerpt, but the implication is clear: the verse ends with 'Glorified is He above what they associate' (as the full verse reads), which negates their corporeal interpretation. The mention of 'shin' and 'fingers' is an exaggerated claim that the author intends to reject as a false attribution of Allah. The passage thus exposes the absurdity of the Mushabbiha position by presenting it alongside the Quranic verse that actually asserts Allah's transcendence.

'Qabd' in the Qur'an means possession and power, not a physical hand.

Refuting Anthropomorphism: The True Meaning of 'Saaq'
Nasafi refutes literal 'saaq' and interprets it as a great matter or Hell's shin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the literal interpretation of God's 'shin' and explains its true meaning as a severe trial or a part of Hell.

Core Idea
The 'shin' of Allah is not a limb but a metaphor for a great event.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a common anthropomorphic claim based on the Quranic verse 'The day the shin shall be uncovered.' He demonstrates that proper tafsir requires interpreting such expressions metaphorically, consistent with divine transcendence. Two valid interpretations are presented: a great matter or a reference to Hell's shin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the opponents' proofs, then two answers.
Pay attention to the types of proofs cited: one verse and two hadiths.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
List the three texts they used: verse 42 of Al-Qalam, and the two hadiths about fingers and foot. Why do they think these imply God has limbs?
3
Analyze Nasafi's first answer
Nasafi interprets 'saaq' as a great and difficult matter. How does this avoid anthropomorphism? Consider the context of the verse (Day of Judgment).
4
Examine the alternative interpretation
Some scholars say it means Hell's shin. How does that also avoid attributing a limb to God? Reflect on the hadith about Hell's structure.
Both interpretations uphold tawhid.
5
Take the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your understanding.

وهم احتجوا في إثبات الساق(۱) الله تعالى بقوله تعالى: ﴿يَوْمَ يُكْشَفُ عَن سَاقٍ﴾ [القلم: ٤٢] وفي الخبر: «إن قلوب العباد بين أصبعي الرحمن يقلبها كيف يشاء» (۲) وفي الخبر: «إنَّ جهنم تقول هل من مزيد، فيضع الربُّ قدمه فيها فتقول: قَطُّ قَطُّ» (۳) يعني: حسبي حسبي.

He also mentions an alternative interpretation from some scholars that the shin refers to the shin of Hell itself, citing a detailed hadith about Hell's enormous structure. Both interpretations avoid attributing a literal shin to God.

The Two Fingers: Interpreting the Hadith of Divine Control
Nasafi explains the hadith 'hearts between two fingers' as between two divine effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'two fingers' in the hadith refer to two divine effects (tawfiq and khidhlan), not literal body parts.

Core Idea
The 'two fingers' are not limbs but divine acts of guidance and abandonment.

What does it mean that our hearts are between the 'fingers' of the Most Merciful? Nasafi, drawing on the linguist al-Asma'i, clarifies that this is a metaphorical expression. The 'fingers' represent two of Allah's effects: tawfiq (granting success) and khidhlan (withholding success). This interpretation preserves divine transcendence while explaining human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Nasafi's explanation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi immediately clarifies the term 'fingers'.
The hadith is well-known; focus on the interpretive move.
2
Identify the key linguistic authority
Nasafi cites al-Asma'i, a renowned linguist, to support that 'finger' here means 'effect'.
This shows the importance of language in theology.
3
Understand the two effects
Learn what tawfiq and khidhlan mean and how they relate to human obedience and disobedience.
Tawfiq leads to obedience; khidhlan leads to disobedience.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this interpretation is important for avoiding anthropomorphism and affirming divine control.
This is a key principle in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Interpreting God's Foot: Precedence, Not Body Parts
Nasafi interprets the hadith of God's foot via linguistic correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi reinterprets the hadith about God's foot to avoid anthropomorphism, showing the correct reading changes the meaning to 'precedence'.

Core Idea
God's 'foot' means precedence, not a body part.

The hadith 'Allah places His foot in Hell' might seem to attribute a physical foot to God. However, by examining the correct pronunciation, Nasafi shows that the intended meaning is 'precedence' or 'foremost among the disbelievers.' This lesson clarifies that subtle but crucial linguistic distinction that safeguards divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and the literal translation. Notice how the author immediately addresses the pronunciation issue.
2
Identify the key term
Focus on the word 'قدم' (qadam/qidam). The passage states that the correct recitation has a kasra on the qaf, making it 'qidam' (precedence).
This shift from fatha to kasra changes the entire meaning.
3
Understand the reinterpretation
Nasafi explains that 'qidam' here means those who preceded in their knowledge among the disbelievers. The hadith does not refer to a literal foot but to a group of people.
4
Reflect on theological importance
Consider why this interpretation matters: it avoids attributing a physical body part to Allah, preserving His transcendence (tanzih).
This is a standard method in kalam: reconciling ambiguous texts with established principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى الخبر: يضع الجبار «قدمه بكسر القاف، وهو الصحيح من الروايات، معناه من كان في قدم علمه من الكفار، والله الهادي (٢).
And the meaning of the report: 'The Almighty places His foot' — with a kasra on the qaf (i.e., qidam), and that is the correct one among the narrations. Its meaning: those who were in the precedence (qidam) of their knowledge among the disbelievers. And God is the Guide (2).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage addresses the hadith about God placing His 'qadam' (foot) in Hell. Nasafi notes that the correct reading is with a kasra on the qaf, making it 'qidam,' which means precedence or advancement. Thus, the hadith does not refer to a literal foot but to those disbelievers who preceded in knowledge or were foremost in their state. This interpretation avoids attributing a physical foot to God. Nasafi ends with 'wa Allahu al-Hadi' (God is the Guide), acknowledging that true guidance is from God.

Key Terms
قدم (qadam/qidam)
كسر القاف (kasr al-qaf)
سبق (sabq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith literally says God has a foot.
Correction: Nasafi shows the correct pronunciation yields 'qidam' (precedence), referring to the disbelievers who preceded in knowledge, not a body part.

Checkpoint

D) As the power of God

Key Takeaway
The correct reading of 'qadam' with a kasra transforms its meaning from a literal foot to precedence, safeguarding God's transcendence.

?

Why Allah Cannot Be Described with Coming and Going
Nasafi argues against describing Allah with coming and going.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why coming and going are negated from Allah and how Nasafi uses Prophetic evidence and Quranic interpretation to prove divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Movement is a sign of being created; Allah is beyond such attributes.

Nasafi addresses a common theological question: can Allah be described with physical movement like coming and going? He firmly states it is impermissible, because these are attributes of created beings. To drive the point home, he invokes Prophet Ibrahim's reasoning and provides an interpretation of a key Quranic verse that might otherwise seem to suggest movement. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp Nasafi's flow.
Notice how he begins with a firm ruling (la yajuz) then supports it.
2
Understand the rationale
Identify why coming and going are negated: because they are attributes of created beings (sifat al-makhluqin) and signs of contingent things (amarat al-muhdathin).
3
Analyze the proof from Ibrahim
Consider how Ibrahim's argument about the setting stars (al-afilun) proves that movement negates divinity. Why is 'setting' evidence that something is not a lord?
4
Examine the Quranic interpretation
Look at how Nasafi interprets 'And your Lord comes' (al-Fajr 22) as meaning 'your Lord's command came'. This is key to defending divine transcendence against anthropomorphism.
This shows the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) for ambiguous verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
لا يجوز وصف الله تعالى بالمجيء والذهاب ]

(۱) ورد بالنسخة (أ) ما نصه: «الأمارات جمع أمارة وهي العلامة».
(۲) ما بين المعقوفتين زيادة من (ب).
(۳) في (ب): المتنقل.
Chapter
It is not permissible to describe Allah Almighty with coming and going.
And it is not permissible that Allah Almighty be described with coming and going, because coming and going are attributes of created beings and signs of contingent things, and they are two attributes negated from Allah Almighty. Do you not see how Ibrahim, peace be upon him, used being one who moves from place to place as evidence that he is not a lord when he said: 'When it set, he said: I do not love those that set' [al-An'am 6:76]. And the meaning of His statement, exalted: 'And your Lord comes and the angels rank on rank' [al-Fajr 22] is 'your Lord's command came.' And His statement, exalted: (fa-anahumu Allah) [?] ...
(1) In copy (A): 'al-amarat is the plural of amara, which is the sign.'
(2) What is between brackets is an addition from (B).
(3) In (B): al-mutanaqqil (the mover).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal proof. The author begins by stating that it is not permissible to describe Allah with coming or going, because these are attributes of created beings and signs of contingent things. He then provides a proof from the story of Prophet Ibrahim, who used the setting of celestial bodies to argue that they cannot be gods: 'I do not love those that set' (Quran 6:76). Setting implies movement, and movement is a sign of being created. Therefore, since Allah is not created, He does not move from place to place. The author then interprets the Quranic verse 'And your Lord comes' (al-Fajr 22) as meaning 'your Lord's command came', thereby rejecting a literal interpretation that would imply physical movement. The passage then begins to cite another verse 'فأنهم الله' but is cut off. The footnotes clarify that 'amarat' means signs, and note manuscript variants.

Key Terms
المجيء (al-maji')
الذهاب (al-dhahab)
الصفات (al-sifāt)
المتنقل (al-mutanaqqil)
الأفول (al-ufūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah literally comes and goes from place to place.
Correction: Nasafi explains that such attributes are only for created beings; Quranic references to 'coming' are interpreted as meaning His command or judgment, not physical movement.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi use the example of Prophet Ibrahim in this passage?
A) To show that setting stars prove Allah's existence
B) To prove that movement is a sign of contingency, negating divinity
C) To argue that Allah also sets
D) To interpret a dream

Key Takeaway
Movement is a property of contingent beings, so Allah, the Eternal, is absolutely free from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about Ibrahim rejecting the setting star, have you ever had a moment in your life when you realized something you loved or relied on was temporary? Did that lead you to turn more fully to Allah, who never 'sets'? How can you turn that insight into a daily practice?
?
The verse 'And your Lord comes' speaks of the Day of Judgment. Are you living today as if His command could come at any moment? What is one specific way you can prepare for that coming—not a physical movement, but a change of heart—starting tonight?
?
If you fully believed that Allah is beyond all movement and change, would you trust Him the same way in the ups and downs of your life? Name one situation where you are doubting His constancy because of your changing circumstances, and commit to a single action that reaffirms your trust in the Unchanging One.

Allah Is Beyond Coming and Going
Nasafi states the rule and interprets anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why coming and going are impossible for Allah and how to interpret Qur'anic verses and hadith that seem to describe Allah moving.

Core Idea
Movement is a sign of originated beings, not the Eternal Creator.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by stating a fundamental rule of Islamic theology: Allah cannot be described with coming and going. He then provides a rational argument from the story of Prophet Ibrahim and interprets several verses and a famous hadith that might appear to contradict this rule. This lesson will help you see how the Salaf understood these texts while preserving God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Nasafi moves from a general rule to specific scriptural proofs.
2
Understand the rational proof
Nasafi uses Ibrahim's statement about the setting celestial body. Understand how that proves that movement negates lordship.
This is a qiyas al-awla (superior analogy).
3
Examine each verse interpretation
Nasafi interprets four verses. For each, identify the literal phrase about 'coming' and how he explains it metaphorically.
All interpretations refer the action to Allah's command or effect, not a physical movement.
4
Consider the hadith about descent
The hadith of Allah's descent to the lowest heaven is also interpreted. Note that Nasafi does not discuss the full details but gives the gist.
This is consistent with the method of tafwid or ta'wil.
5
Reflect on the method
Nasafi uses ta'wil (interpretation) to reconcile scriptural expressions with rational principles. Ask yourself why this is necessary.
This protects tanzih (transcendence) without denying the texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز أن يُوصف الله تعالى بالمجيء والذهاب؛ لأن المجيء والذهاب من صفات المخلوقين وأمارات المحدثين، وهما صفتان منتفيتان عن الله تعالى، ألا ترى أن إبراهيم صلوات الله عليه وسلم كيف استدل بالمتنقل من مكان إلى مكان أنه ليس برب حيث قال: ﴿فَلَمَّا أَفَلَ قَالَ لا أُحِبُّ الأَفِلِينَ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٦] ومعنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ وَالْمَلَكُ صَفَّا صَفَّا﴾ [الفجر: ٢٢] أي أمر ربك. وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَنَهُمُ اللَّهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ لَمْ يَحْتَسِبُوا﴾ [الحشر: ٢] يعني: قتل كعب بن الأشرف لعنه الله. وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَتَى اللَّهُ بُنْيَانَهُم مِّنَ الْقَوَاعِدِ﴾ [النحل: ٢٦] يعني استهلكهم واستأصلهم فلم يبق منهم نافخ نار ولا ساكن ديار، نزلت في غزو نمرود بن كنعان لعنهم الله. ومعنى قوله تعالى: ﴿هَلْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَّا أَن يَأْتِيَهُمُ اللَّهُ فِي ظُلَلٍ مِّنَ الْغَمَامِ﴾ [البقرة: ٢١] يعني بعدما أثبتنا من الدلائل أنه لا شبيه له، ولا شريك له، ولا مجيء له، ينظرون إتيانه في ظلل من الغمام ويعتقدون هذا ليؤمنوا به، وهذا في صفات الله تعالى محال. ومعنى الخبر: ينزل الله تعالى كل ليلة النصف من شعبان إلى السماء الدنيا فيقول: هل من تائب فيتاب عليه.
It is not permissible to describe Allah Almighty with coming and going, because coming and going are from the attributes of created beings and signs of originated things, and they are two attributes negated from Allah Almighty. Do you not see how Abraham, peace be upon him, argued by means of moving from place to place that he is not a lord, when he said: 'When it set, he said, I do not love those that set' [Al-An'am 6:76]? And the meaning of His saying, the Exalted: 'And your Lord comes and the angels rank upon rank' [Al-Fajr 89:22] is 'the command of your Lord'. And His saying, the Exalted: 'So Allah came upon them from where they did not expect' [Al-Hashr 59:2] means: He killed Ka'b ibn al-Ashraf, may Allah curse him. And His saying, the Exalted: 'So Allah ruined their buildings from the foundations' [An-Nahl 16:26] means: He destroyed and uprooted them until no blower of fire nor dweller of houses remained of them; it was revealed concerning the campaign of Nimrod bin Canaan, may Allah curse them. And the meaning of His saying, the Exalted: 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds' [Al-Baqarah 2:210] means: after we have established by proofs that He has no likeness, no partner, and no coming, they look for His coming in canopies of clouds and believe this in order to believe in Him, and this regarding the attributes of Allah is impossible. And the meaning of the report: 'Allah Almighty descends every night of mid-Sha'ban to the lowest heaven and says: Is there any repentant that he may be forgiven?'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument on divine transcendence. The author begins by stating the rule: it is not permissible to describe Allah with coming and going (al-maji' wa al-dhahab). The reason given is that coming and going are attributes of created beings (sifat al-makhluqin) and signs of originated things (amarat al-muhdathin). These two attributes are negated from Allah. The author then provides a proof from the Quranic story of Ibrahim (AS). When Ibrahim saw a celestial body set, he used its movement from one place to another to argue that it could not be a lord, saying 'I do not love those that set.' The author argues that if movement disproves lordship for a created body, how much more does it negate any form of movement for Allah. Next, the author interprets Quranic verses that seem to ascribe coming to Allah. For the verse 'And your Lord comes' (Surah Al-Fajr), he explains it means 'the command of your Lord came.' Similarly, 'So Allah came upon them' (Surah Al-Hashr) is interpreted as the killing of Ka'b ibn Ashraf—an act of divine punishment, not a physical movement. The verse about destroying the building from the foundations (Surah An-Nahl) is explained as complete annihilation of Nimrod's people. The verse about Allah coming in canopies of clouds (Surah Al-Baqarah) is said to be impossible in relation to Allah's attributes; the disbelievers' expectation of such a thing is futile because Allah has already established proofs that He has no likeness, partner, or coming. Finally, the author addresses the hadith about Allah's descent to the lowest heaven on the night of mid-Sha'ban. He gives its meaning as a descent of mercy, not a physical movement, and quotes the hadith where Allah says 'Is there any repentant...' The author's consistent method is ta'wil (interpretation) of anthropomorphic expressions to protect divine transcendence.

Key Terms
المجيء والذهاب (al-maji' wa al-dhahab)
صفات المخلوقين (sifat al-makhluqin)
أمارات المحدثين (amarat al-muhdathin)
تعطيل (ta'til)
تأويل (ta'wil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Qur'anic verses about Allah's 'coming' or 'descent' are literal, implying physical movement.
Correction: Nasafi insists that these expressions must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid attributing physical movement to Allah, as movement is a sign of createdness.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, what is the meaning of the verse 'And your Lord comes' (Surah Al-Fajr 89:22)?
A) Allah physically descends from His throne
B) The command and punishment of your Lord comes
C) Allah moves from place to place
D) The angels carry Allah's throne

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely free from physical movement, and any scriptural expression suggesting otherwise must be interpreted in a way befitting His majesty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'Your Lord comes' or 'Allah descends', do you catch yourself forming a mental image of movement or location? What does that say about how deeply you have internalized tanzih?
?
Abraham rejected a celestial body as lord because it moved. Are there 'moving' things in your life—your career, relationships, possessions—that you treat as ultimate? How does this lesson expose a hidden shirk in your heart?
?
If you truly believed Allah is beyond space and time, how would it change the way you pray—your sense of being heard, your focus, your urgency? Would you pray differently starting today?

God's Coming in Qur'an 2:210: A Metaphorical Interpretation
Imam Nasafi explains the metaphorical meaning of God's coming in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi interprets the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in the shadows of clouds?' as metaphorical, based on the established proofs of God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's coming in the Qur'an is metaphorical, not literal.

When we encounter verses that describe God in seemingly physical terms, we must reconcile them with the definitive proofs of divine transcendence. Imam Nasafi here addresses one such verse, explaining that after establishing that God has no likeness, partner, or movement, the 'coming' mentioned in the verse cannot be literal. Instead, it points to a symbolic event that people await as a sign of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the passage
Start by reading the Qur'anic verse (2:210) and then the Arabic passage from Imam Nasafi. Notice how he refers back to previously established proofs.
The verse is: 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in the shadows of clouds?'
2
Understand the prior proofs
Imam Nasafi reminds us that we have already proven that God has no likeness, no partner, and no literal movement. This sets the foundation for why a literal interpretation is impossible.
These proofs are part of the broader argument for divine transcendence (tanzih).
3
Identify the conclusion
He then states that the 'coming' (ityan) in the verse is what people await, but a literal interpretation is impossible (muhal) in God's attributes.
The term 'muhal' is a strong negation in kalam.
4
Reflect on the metaphorical meaning
Consider what the verse might mean metaphorically—perhaps the manifestation of God's power or a sign of judgment. The author does not specify the metaphor here, but the key point is that it is not literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى قوله تعالى: ﴿هَلْ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَّا أَن يَأْتِيَهُمُ اللَّهُ فِي ظُلَلٍ مِّنَ الْغَمَامِ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢١] يعني بعدما أثبتنا من الدلائل أنه لا شبيه له، ولا شريك له، ولا مجيء له، ينظرون إتيانه في ظلل من الغمام ويعتقدون هذا ليؤمنوا به، وهذا في صفاتِ الله تعالى محالٌ.
And the meaning of His statement, the Exalted: 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in the shadows of clouds?' [Al-Baqarah 2:210] means: after we have proven through evidences that He has no likeness, no partner, and no coming (maji'), they await His coming (ityan) in the shadows of clouds and believe this in order to have faith in Him, and this is impossible (muhal) in the attributes of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of a potentially problematic verse about God's 'coming.' The author states that we have already established through proofs that God has no likeness, partner, or literal movement. Therefore, the 'coming' mentioned in the verse must be interpreted metaphorically—people await a sign, not a literal divine arrival. The author concludes that a literal interpretation is impossible (muhal) with respect to God's attributes.

Key Terms
إتيان (ityan)
مجيء (maji')
محال (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Qur'an 2:210 affirms that God literally moves and descends in clouds.
Correction: Imam Nasafi clarifies that based on definitive proofs of divine transcendence, literal movement is impossible. The verse must be interpreted metaphorically, referring to a sign or manifestation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi interprets God's coming in Qur'an 2:210 as a metaphorical sign, not a literal event, because prior proofs establish that God has no physical attributes or movement.

?

Interpreting the Hadith of God's Descent on Mid-Sha'ban
Imam Nasafi interprets the night of mid-Sha'ban descent as God's attention and mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi interprets the hadith of God's descent on the night of mid-Sha'ban as a metaphor for divine attention and mercy, preserving God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'descent' is not a journey; it is His turning with mercy toward His servants.

The hadith of God's descent in the middle of Sha'ban might seem ambiguous. Imam Nasafi clarifies that this 'descent' (nuzul) does not imply physical movement. Instead, it means Allah directs His attention and mercy to His servants. This lesson explains the reasoning and scriptural analogy that support this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and interpretation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The hadith mentions God descending to the lowest heaven. Notice how Nasafi reinterprets 'nuzul'.
The hadith is quoted, then explained.
2
Understand the key term 'nuzul'
Nasafi defines 'nuzul' as 'اطلاع وإقبال' (attention and turning). This is the core of the interpretation.
This preserves God's transcendence.
3
Examine the Quranic analogy
Nasafi cites Quran 15:9 about sending down the Reminder. He shows that 'inzal' there means teaching, not literal descent. The same applies here.
This strengthens the metaphorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى الخبر: ينزل الله تعالى كل ليلة النصف من شعبان إلى السماء الدنيا فيقول: هل من تائب فيتاب عليه. قلنا: النزول من الله تعالى الاطلاع والإقبال على عباده، يعني ينظر إلى عباده بالرحمة. هكذا نُقِلَ عن علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه، كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفِظُونَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٩] ولم يُرد به حقيقة الإنزال. معناه: علمناه وفهمناه، كذلك ههنا.
And the meaning of the report: 'Allah descends every night of the middle of Sha'ban to the lowest heaven and says: Is there anyone who repents that I may forgive him.' We said: Descent (nuzul) from Allah is His turning to and attending to His servants, meaning He looks upon His servants with mercy. Thus it has been reported from Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, just as Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We will be its guardian' [Al-Hijr 15:9], and He did not intend by it the literal descent (inzal). Its meaning: We taught it and made it understood. Likewise here.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage addresses the hadith about God's descent on the night of mid-Sha'ban. The author provides the interpretation: 'nuzul' means God's turning attention and mercy towards His servants, not a physical movement. This is supported by citing Ali ibn Abi Talib and an analogy with the verse about 'We sent down the Reminder,' where 'inzal' means teaching, not literal descent.

Key Terms

إنزال (inzal)
تأويل (ta'wil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might interpret the hadith literally, believing God physically descends.
Correction: Imam Nasafi explains that 'nuzul' is a metaphor for God's attentive mercy, not a physical motion.

D) As a symbol of punishment.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi interprets God's 'descent' as a metaphor for His mercy and attention, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Descent as Mercy: A Figurative Interpretation
Defines nuzul figuratively and gives Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of divine descent (nuzul) must be interpreted figuratively to preserve Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
When Allah 'descends,' He turns to His servants with mercy.

Scripture describes Allah as 'descending,' but how can this be understood without implying spatial movement? In this passage, the author provides a classical interpretation that safeguards divine transcendence: 'nuzul' is not a literal descent but an act of mercy and attention. This lesson unpacks the evidence and reasoning behind this figurative understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice how it begins with 'We said' – this is a response to an implied objection. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is concise but packed with key terms.
2
Identify the figurative definition
Find the words used to define 'nuzul' (al-ittila' and al-iqbal). Understand that these denote divine attention and mercy, not physical movement.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Look at the verse from Surah Al-Hijr. The author argues that 'nuzul' here means teaching, not literal descent. See how this supports the figurative interpretation.
This is a key analogical argument.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that nuzul implies literal movement. How does this passage correct that?
This protection of divine transcendence is central to kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: النزول من الله تعالى الاطلاع والإقبال على عباده، يعني ينظر إلى عباده بالرحمة. هكذا نُقِلَ عن علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه، كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفِظُونَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٩] ولم يُرد به حقيقة الإنزال. معناه: علمناه وفهمناه، كذلك ههنا.
We said: The 'descending' (nuzul) from Allah the Exalted is [His] overlooking and turning toward His servants, meaning He looks at His servants with mercy. Thus it was transmitted from Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, just as Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We will preserve it' [Al-Hijr 15:9] and He did not intend thereby a literal sending down. Its meaning: We taught it and made it understood. Likewise here.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a definition and interpretive explanation. It begins by defining the meaning of 'nuzul' (divine descent) in a way consistent with divine transcendence. The author states that nuzul from Allah is actually 'al-ittila'' (overseeing) and 'al-iqbal' (turning toward) His servants, meaning He looks upon them with mercy. This interpretation is attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib, providing an authoritative early precedent. The author then supports this figurative interpretation by citing Quran 15:9, where Allah says 'We have sent down the Reminder' but does not mean a literal descent; rather, it means 'We taught it and made it understood.' The author concludes by analogy: 'Likewise here' – just as the descent of the Reminder is metaphorical, so too must the attributive descent of Allah be understood metaphorically.

Key Terms
النزول
الاطلاع
الإقبال
الإنزال
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'nuzul' implies literal spatial movement for Allah.
Correction: The passage explains that nuzul means 'overlooking and turning with mercy,' not physical descent.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the term 'nuzul' when attributed to Allah?
A) As a literal descent from a higher place.
B) As an increase in knowledge.
C) As His overlooking and turning toward His servants with mercy.
D) As a reward for fasting on the 15th of Sha‘ban.

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of 'nuzul' is not literal movement but a figurative expression of Allah's merciful attention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the hadith about Allah's descent, does your heart immediately imagine a spatial movement, or do you pause to sense the mercy that the phrase is meant to convey? What does your immediate reaction reveal about the state of your faith?
?
The verse 'We have sent down the Reminder' is understood as 'We taught it.' Are there other verses or attributes you have been reading literally without seeking the deeper meaning? Will you commit today to studying the principles of tanzih so that you worship Allah as He truly is, not as your imagination pictures Him?
?
If Allah 'descends' specifically to offer mercy, how should that change the way you turn to Him in the last third of the night? Are you actually expecting His mercy, or are you just waiting for a ritual?

Refuting the Claim That Allah Is a Composite Body
Refutes the claim that Allah is a composite body and upholds tawheed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Allah is a composite body contradicts tawheed and leads to disbelief.

Core Idea
If Allah had parts, He would not be one God—so composition is impossible.

In this lesson, we examine a classical objection: 'What if we say Allah is a composite body? Does it harm us?' The author (Imam Nasafi) responds with a forceful argument rooted in the definition of 'body' and the clear Qur'anic declaration of God's oneness. You'll see how a single theological error can unravel the entire foundation of tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection (fa'in qīla) and the author's sharp response (qulnā).
The word 'jism' (body) is defined as 'compound' (murakkab) and 'composed' (mu'allaf).
2
Understand the definition of 'body'
The author argues that 'body' necessarily implies composition of parts. Therefore, affirming a body for Allah means affirming parts for Him.
3
Trace the consequence of parts
If Allah had parts, each part would act independently in creation, provision, etc., leading to multiple gods.
The Qur'anic verse (2:163) is quoted to prove that God is one.
4
Analyze the two scenarios
The author considers two cases: (a) all parts are divine, (b) only some parts are divine. Both lead to disbelief.
The second scenario mixes Creator and created.
5
Internalize the lesson on tawheed
Reflect on how this argument protects the absolute oneness of Allah. Any notion of composition is incompatible with true tawheed.
This is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو قلنا بأن الله تعالى جسماً مركباً ليس يضرنا. قلنا: يضركم؛ لأن الجسم عبارة عن مركب ومؤلف، فإذا أثبتم الأبعاض فقد قلتم بأنه لا يكون إلهاً واحداً، وقال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِلَهُكُمْ إِلَهُ وَاحِدٌ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٣]. وإذا أنكرتم النصَّ فقد كفرتم، لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يحصل التخليق والترزيق والإحداث والاختراع لكل جزء منه، ولكل عضو منه، فيؤدي إلى أن لا يكون إلها واحدا، ومن قال هذا يكفر. وإذا قلتم بأن بعض أجزائه إله، وبعض أجزائه ليس بإله، يكون هذا جمعا بين الخالق والمخلوق والرازق والمرزوق ومن قال هذا يكفر.
If it is said: If we say that Allah the Exalted is a composite body, that does not harm us. We say: It harms you; because 'body' (jism) is an expression of a composite and composed thing. So if you affirm parts, you have said that He is not one God. And Allah the Exalted said: 'And your God is one God' [Al-Baqarah 2:163]. And if you deny the text, you have disbelieved, because it leads to the result that creation, provision, origination, and invention occur for each part of Him and each member of Him, leading to Him not being one God. And whoever says this disbelieves. And if you say that some of His parts are God and some are not God, this is combining the Creator and the created, and the Provider and the provided. And whoever says this disbelieves.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah is a composite body (jism). It follows an objection-and-answer format and is logically structured. First, an interlocutor objects: 'If we say Allah is a composite body, that does not harm us.' The author responds forcefully: 'It harms you.' The reason given is that 'body' inherently means a composite of parts. If one affirms parts for Allah, then one has implicitly denied that He is one God, contradicting the clear Qur’anic verse (2:163) that declares God is one. Moreover, denying that clear text (nass) constitutes disbelief (kufr). The author then explains the consequence: if Allah had parts, then each part would have its own act of creation, provision, etc., which would lead to multiple gods—a clear violation of tawheed. The argument then extends: even if one says only some parts are divine and others not, that still combines Creator and created, which is also disbelief. Thus, the author firmly rejects any notion of composition in Allah, preserving the absolute oneness required by tawheed.

مركب
مؤلف
أبعاض
إله واحد
تخليق
ترزيق
إحداث
اختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can say Allah is a composite body without harming tawheed.
Correction: The author shows that 'body' inherently means composite, leading to parts, which contradicts the Qur'anic declaration of oneness and leads to disbelief.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for rejecting the claim that Allah is a composite body?
A) Because the word 'jism' is not used in the Quran.
B) Because it implies parts, leading to multiple gods and denying clear Quranic verses.
C) Because the Prophet never described Allah as a body.
D) Because it contradicts the hadith about descent.

Key Takeaway
Affirming any composition in Allah contradicts the Qur'anic declaration of His oneness and results in disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that affirming any composition in Allah leads to disbelief. In your own heart, have you ever subtly pictured Allah as having 'parts'—like a hand, an eye, or a location? What real change must you make today to ensure your concept of Allah is free from any trace of composition?
?
If someone told you that a respected local teacher believes Allah has a body, would you have the courage to correct them using the evidence from this passage? Are you willing to speak the truth even if it costs you a relationship?
?
The passage says that if you deny the clear text of the Qur'an, you disbelieve. Examine your daily life: where do you prioritize your own reasoning or culture over the plain meaning of the Qur'an? What is one step you can take today to submit more fully to Allah's word?
